It <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/powerpost/at-parades-and-protests-senators-get-earful-about-health-care/2017/07/04/ab675938-5de2-11e7-9fc6-c7ef4bc58d13_story.html?utm_term=.956ed64997e1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">helped halt</a> the Republican drive to repeal the Affordable Care Act \u2014 peeling critical support away from Mitch McConnell, the Senate majority leader \u2014 and last year powered an electoral wave that gave the Democratic Party its first majority in the House of Representatives since 2010.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If Democrats can be confident ahead of 2020, it's because of grass-roots activists who have strengthened the party's political position time and time again. And yet key Democratic leaders are still reluctant to follow their lead. </p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">[Jamelle Bouie answered your questions about this column </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/jbouie/status/1138153874521436160\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">on Twitter</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.]</em></p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">We've seen this in the fight over impeachment. <!-- -->Many Democrats \u2014 36 percen<!-- -->t <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.npr.org/2019/06/08/730697885/poll-support-for-impeachment-hearings-grows-but-americans-split-on-way-forward\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">according to a recent poll</a> from Marist and NPR \u2014 want Speaker Nancy Pelosi and other leaders to begin impeachment proceedings. Those <!-- -->who don't still want <!-- -->Democrats to continue to pursue investigations into the president's wrongdoing. Constituents in moderate and liberal districts are urging their lawmakers to hold the president accountable for his actions. But Pelosi won't budge. She and her allies insist impeachment is unnecessary, unpopular and divisive. </p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"I think we're going to do what's right for the country and at this point, the speaker hasn't reached the conclusion and I haven't <!-- --> either<!-- -->,\" Representative Adam Schiff, chairman of the House Intelligence Committee, said last week. \"It's not best for the country to put us through an impeachment proceeding that's destined for a failure in the Senate.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">There was a similar dynamic at work in the fight to keep Brett Kavanaugh off the Supreme Court. In his new book, \"We've Got People: From Jesse Jackson to AOC, the End of Big Money and the Rise of a Movement,\" Ryan Grim, Washington bureau chief for the Intercept, describes how Senate Minority Leader Chuck Schumer and Senator Dianne Feinstein resisted activist pressure to escalate the fight against Kavanaugh,<!-- --> with Schumer \"repeatedly\" telling outside allies that \"a furious stand against Kavanaugh would enrage Trump supporters and only disappoint progressive voters.\" <!-- -->Democratic leaders thought Kavanaugh was so damaged by the revelations and testimony of Christine Blasey Ford that further action was unnecessary. The grass roots could be ignored. Kavanaugh, of course, was confirmed, 50 to 48.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-1ef8w8q e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><img alt=\"\" class=\"css-1m50asq\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" itemprop=\"url\" sizes=\"((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 80vw, 100vw\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" srcset=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/10/opinion/10bouie2/merlin_153304917_a06dbdb3-10c2-47f0-ad98-0ff7e2674fa4-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w\"/></div><figcaption class=\"css-18crmh6 e1xdpqjp0\" itemprop=\"caption description\"><span class=\"css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0\">The remains of an anti-Kavanaugh poster in Washington.</span><span class=\"css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90\" itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Credit</span><span>Damon Winter/The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/jamellebouie?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">[Join Jamelle Bouie as he shines a light on overlooked writing, culture and ideas from around the internet. For exclusive thoughts, photos and reading recommendations, sign up for his newsletter.]</em></a></p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">You can read these simply as tactical and strategic quarrels. But I think there's a deeper divide, between a politics that sees the grass roots as an asset to use and cultivate versus one that treats it as a complication to manage. It's a \"leader knows best\" approach that may squander the Democratic Party's advantage of enthusiasm and drive against a corrupt and unpopular president. It even seems as though for Democrats like Pelosi, the only political actions that truly matter are either in the legislature or at the ballot box. It's an understandable view for a leadership class whose political memories stretch back to the devastating Democratic losses of 1968 and 1972 (to say nothing of 1980 and 1984). But <!-- -->it's also bred of a deep aversion <!-- -->to risk-taking, even when circumstances warrant bold action.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If the Trump era has revealed anything about the state of American politics, it's that the realm of possibility is far greater than previously recognized. There are still limits and obstacles, but there are also opportunities to fundamentally shift the terms of political conflict and debate. There are Democrats outside of the grass roots who understand this and have adopted a tactical and strategic boldness that suits the moment.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Backed by the base of the party, liberal candidates like Doug Jones in Alabama and Stacey Abrams in Georgia have run aggressive campaigns in ostensibly hostile territory, making unheard-of gains in the process. Presidential contenders like Bernie Sanders and Elizabeth Warren have aimed their campaigns at that grass-roots sense of possibility with expansive policy programs and a transformative vision. Exemplified by Representative Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez, younger lawmakers are speaking to and amplifying the grass roots, building powerful public platforms in the process. But Democratic leaders in Congress remain skeptical, seemingly stuck in an era when Democrats were on the defensive and backlash politics ruled the day.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Then again, Pelosi and Schumer are shrewd politicians with decades of experience. Perhaps their resistance to grass-roots Democrats, and to impeachment in particular, will pay dividends. But we should consider the reverse as well: that a Democratic Party that plays with excessive caution \u2014 and keeps its base at a distance \u2014 is one that might demobilize its voters and <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/03/10/opinion/sunday/obama-trump-voters-democrats.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">produce the same conditions</a> that helped Trump win in the first place.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-g92qtk epkadsg3\"><div class=\"css-1owp1gq epkadsg0\">More on Mobilizing Democrats</div><div class=\"css-15g2oxy epkadsg2\"><div class=\"css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/03/opinion/impeachment-trump.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article\"><div class=\"css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4\"><div class=\"css-14b9hti e16ij5yr5\">Opinion | Michelle Goldberg</div><div class=\"css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2\">Democratic Voters Want Impeachment. The House Dawdles.</div><time class=\"css-rqb9bm e16638kd0\" datetime=\"2019-06-03\">June 3, 2019</time></div><div class=\"css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0\"><img class=\"css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/06/opinion/03goldbergWeb/03goldbergWeb-threeByTwoSmallAt2X.jpg\"/></div></a></div><div class=\"css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/03/10/opinion/sunday/obama-trump-voters-democrats.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article\"><div class=\"css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4\"><div class=\"css-14b9hti e16ij5yr5\">Opinion | Sean McElwee, Jesse H. Rhodes, Brian F. Schaffner and Bernard L. Fraga</div><div class=\"css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2\">The Missing Obama Millions</div><time class=\"css-rqb9bm e16638kd0\" datetime=\"2018-03-10\">March 10, 2018</time></div><div class=\"css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0\"><img class=\"css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/03/11/sunday-review/11McElwee/11McElwee-threeByTwoSmallAt2X-v3.jpg\"/></div></a></div><div class=\"css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/13/opinion/pelosi-trump-civil-war.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article\"><div class=\"css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4\"><div class=\"css-14b9hti e16ij5yr5\">Opinion | Jamelle Bouie</div><div class=\"css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2\">What Democrats Can Learn About Impeachment From the Civil War</div><time class=\"css-rqb9bm e16638kd0\" datetime=\"2019-05-13\">May 13, 2019</time></div><div class=\"css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0\"><img class=\"css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/05/16/opinion/13bouieWeb/13bouieWeb-threeByTwoSmallAt2X.jpg\"/></div></a></div></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-jwz2nf etfikam0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">The Times is committed to publishing </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/01/31/opinion/letters/letters-to-editor-new-york-times-women.html\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">a diversity of letters</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> to the editor. We'd like to hear what you think about this or any of our articles. Here are some </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://help.nytimes.com/hc/en-us/articles/115014925288-How-to-submit-a-letter-to-the-editor\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">tips</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">. And here's our email: </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"mailto:letters@nytimes.com\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">letters@nytimes.com</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.</em></p><p class=\"css-jwz2nf etfikam0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Follow The New York Times Opinion section on </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.facebook.com/nytopinion\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Facebook</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">, </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"http://twitter.com/NYTOpinion\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Twitter (@NYTopinion)</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> and </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.instagram.com/nytopinion/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Instagram</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.</em></p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div></section>", "link": "/2019/06/10/opinion/resistance-democrats-pelosi-schumer.html", "title": "<title data-rh=\"true\">Opinion | Pelosi and Schumer Are Playing Too Nice - The New York Times</title>"}, "sudan-protests": {"body": "<section class=\"meteredContent css-1i2y565\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" name=\"articleBody\"><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Since the Arab uprisings of 2011, Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates have used their considerable resources to promote authoritarian governments run by military strongmen in the region. They helped <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2011/03/15/world/middleeast/15bahrain.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">crush</a> Bahrain's uprising, bankrolled a <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/09/us/politics/trump-abdel-fattah-el-sisi.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">return</a> to military dictatorship in Egypt, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/26/world/africa/sudan-revolution-protest-saudi-arabia-gulf.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">armed</a> a rogue military leader in Libya and mismanaged a democratic transition in Yemen before launching a <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2018/10/26/world/middleeast/saudi-arabia-war-yemen.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">destructive war</a> there.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">A ghastly new chapter in the Saudi and the Emirati counterrevolution against democratic movements in the region is unfolding in Sudan, whose generals have unleashed terrible violence on supporters of democracy.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">On the morning of June 3, Sudanese armed forces attacked sit-ins in the capital, Khartoum, and elsewhere, killing more than 100 people and injuring over 500. Even though the internet has been largely blocked, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/iyad_elbaghdadi/status/1137467467054374913\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">reports and videos have trickled out</a>: <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/hano_Odii/status/1136599059312066560\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">bloodied protesters</a> being carried away to makeshift first-aid stations; heartbreaking accounts of gang <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/BSonblast/status/1135925972656504832\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">rapes of doctors</a>; disfigured bodies of protesters <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/babamoh6748262/status/1137445144095932416\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">pulled out of the Nile</a>; protesters <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/oxosara/status/1136988816345042949\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">burned</a> in their sit-in tents.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The two men ultimately responsible for the troops who committed these atrocities are Lt. Gen. Abdel Fattah al-Burhan and Lt. Gen. Mohamed Hamdan, known as Hemeti, who are heading the Transitional Military Council that replaced the ousted longtime president, Omar Hassan al-Bashir. General Hamdan leads <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://foreignpolicy.com/2019/05/14/man-who-terrorized-darfur-is-leading-sudans-supposed-transition-hemeti-rsf-janjaweed-bashir-khartoum/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">the Rapid Support Forces</a>, the paramilitary force created by rebranding the <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.france24.com/en/20190605-sudan-military-protest-janjaweed-dagalo-future\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">janjaweed</a> militias that conducted the genocide in Darfur.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The crackdown came after weeks of negotiations between protest leaders and the military council reached an impasse. The protest leaders wanted the military to immediately hand over power to civilians; the council insisted on military rule until <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"http://www.hurriyetdailynews.com/sudans-tmc-calls-for-elections-in-nine-months-143930\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">elections, promised to be held</a> within nine months.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Protesters feared that elections under the military council would neither be free nor fair, but simply a cover to legitimize the appointment of a new military dictator \u2014 essentially repeating what happened in Egypt in 2014 when Field Marshal Abdel Fattah el-Sisi won by 97 percent. Mr. el-Sisi rose to power after <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2013/11/14/world/middleeast/memory-egypt-mass-killing.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">the Egyptian Army killed </a>up to 1,000 largely unarmed protesters at a sit-in at Rabaa Square in Cairo in 2013. Parallels to Egypt were not lost on the Sudanese protesters, who had been <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.albawaba.com/node/%E2%80%98victory-or-egypt%E2%80%99-lessons-arab-spring-loom-large-sudan-1279786\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">chanting</a>, \"<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://uk.reuters.com/article/uk-sudan-politics-hemedti/sudanese-militia-commander-waits-in-wings-after-president-ousted-idUKKCN1RZ0XE\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">Either victory or Egypt</a>.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Sudan's democracy advocates also feared that the very forces that catapulted Mr. el-Sisi to power in Egypt would be pulling the strings in Sudan. Before and after the Rabaa massacre, Egypt received diplomatic support and <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2013/08/20/world/middleeast/saudi-arabia-vows-to-back-egypts-rulers.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">billions of dollars</a> of financial assistance from two wealthy Arab monarchies: Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">General <!-- -->al-<!-- -->Burhan and General Hamdan have had a close working relationship with the Saudi and Emirati leadership since at least 2015, having been <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://jamestown.org/program/sudans-controversial-rapid-support-forces-bolsters-saudi-efforts-yemen/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">directly involved</a> in the Saudi-led war on Yemen. General al-Burhan has been overseeing Sudan's more than 10,000 <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://southfront.org/sudanese-forces-in-yemeni-war/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">ground troops</a> in Yemen. These troops include thousands of militiamen from the <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/28/world/africa/saudi-sudan-yemen-child-fighters.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">Rapid Security Forces</a> headed by General Hamdan.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-g92qtk epkadsg3\"><div class=\"css-1owp1gq epkadsg0\">Related</div><div class=\"css-15g2oxy epkadsg2\"><div class=\"css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/02/world/middleeast/crown-prince-mohammed-bin-zayed.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article\"><div class=\"css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4\"><div class=\"css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2\">The Most Powerful Arab Ruler Isn't M.B.S. It's M.B.Z.</div><time class=\"css-rqb9bm e16638kd0\" datetime=\"2019-06-02\">June 2, 2019</time></div><div class=\"css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0\"><img class=\"css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/02/world/02MBZ3/02MBZ3-threeByTwoSmallAt2X-v3.jpg\"/></div></a></div><div class=\"css-2b3w4o e16ij5yr6\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/07/27/sunday-review/obama-egypt-coup-trump.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article\"><div class=\"css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4\"><div class=\"css-1j8dw05 e16ij5yr2\">The White House and the Strongman</div><time class=\"css-rqb9bm e16638kd0\" datetime=\"2018-07-27\">July 27, 2018</time></div><div class=\"css-1vm5oi9 e16ij5yr0\"><img class=\"css-32rbo2 e16ij5yr1\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/07/28/opinion/sunday/29Kirkpatrick1/merlin_71087167_9393cee1-d8c7-41e5-a0e3-ba70350b439f-threeByTwoSmallAt2X.jpg\"/></div></a></div></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">When Mr. al-Bashir was besieged by popular protests in early April, Sudanese military leaders thought it was time to sideline him. A delegation traveled to Cairo to secure political and financial support from Egypt, Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates, which disapproved of Mr. al-Bashir's ties to Islamists and his outreach to Turkey and Qatar. Egypt, Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates followed up by <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://apnews.com/e30e894617cf4dfb9a811af2df22de93\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">reaching out to General al-Burhan and General Hamdan</a>.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">After the Transitional Military Council assumed power, the Saudis and the Emiratis made their influence soon felt: On April 21, they <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.reuters.com/article/us-sudan-protests/saudi-arabia-uae-to-send-3-billion-in-aid-to-sudan-idUSKCN1RX0DG\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">approved $3 billion</a> in aid to Sudan, <!-- -->in a bid to strengthen the council. <!-- -->Yet in the weeks after, protesters continued to increase the pressure, as the number at sit-in camps swelled even as tense negotiations continued.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">In late May, General Hamdan met with Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman of Saudi Arabia and reaffirmed his <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.thenational.ae/world/mena/sudan-s-military-leadership-pledges-to-back-saudi-arabia-against-iran-threats-1.866068\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">commitment</a> to a close alliance with the kingdom. General al-Burhan met the Mr. el-Sisi in Cairo and the United Arab Emirates' de facto ruler, Mohammed bin Zayed, in Abu Dhabi. They seemed to return home emboldened: They declared the breakdown of negotiations with the Sudanese opposition, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.middleeastmonitor.com/20190531-sudan-military-wont-hand-over-power-to-civilians-until-elections-are-held/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">rejected</a> calls to hand over power, declared the sit-in camps to be \"<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/may/31/sudan-bans-al-jazeera-as-pro-democracy-demonstrations-continue\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">a threat to the national security of the state</a>,\" and banned Al Jazeera from reporting in Sudan.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Then came the June 3 attack on the protesters, which carried echoes of the assault on Bahrain's <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2011/feb/17/bahrain-protests-riot-police-storm-pearl-square\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">Pearl Square</a> protest camp in 2011 and the Egyptian massacre. Videos uploaded on social media by citizen journalists <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/trbrtc/status/1136245203252187136\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">clearly showed</a> Emirati-manufactured military gear deployed by Sudanese militias. Influential Saudi-controlled accounts on Twitter expressed their <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/monther72/status/1136009969915113473\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">support</a> for Sudan's military leaders and <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/monther72/status/1136036512217391105\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">warned</a> against the \"folly\" of popular uprising.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The Saudi and Emirati reactionary role in Sudan and their influence over the Transitional Military Council's actions was highlighted by the<!-- --> <!-- -->reaction from the United States. After the killings, the under secretary of state for political affairs, David Hale, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.state.gov/under-secretary-hales-call-with-saudi-deputy-defense-minister-khalid-bin-salman/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">called</a> the Saudi deputy defense minister Khalid bin Salman and the United Arab Emirates' foreign minister, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.state.gov/under-secretary-hales-call-with-minister-of-state-for-foreign-affairs-anwar-gargash-of-the-united-arab-emirates/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">Anwar Gargash</a>, to express concern over the crackdown and to note \"the importance of a transition to a civilian-led government in accordance with the will of the Sudanese people.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates claim that their interventions have been aimed at staving off Islamist extremism and promoting regional stability. But Sudan's uprising against Mr. al-Bashir is an uprising against political Islamists; the protest leaders who have been negotiating with the military council are not Islamists. Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates are instead driven by their own <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2019/may/05/saudi-arabia-sudan-uprising-omar-al-bashir\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">fear</a> that should a major Arab country transition to democracy, it would lead to upheavals at home.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">With Sudan's Arab neighbors under Saudi and Emirati sway, with the United States doing little beyond expressing concern, it has fallen to Ethiopia's prime minister, Abiy Ahmed, to take the lead in <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/reuters/2019/06/07/world/africa/07reuters-sudan-politics.html?searchResultPosition=5&amp;module=inline\" title=\"\">mediation</a> between the military council and the opposition. And outraged at the killings in Sudan, the African Union has <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.africanews.com/2019/06/06/african-union-suspends-sudan-demands-civilian-led-transition-authority/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">suspended</a> Sudan's membership and called for a civilian-led transitional government.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But as long as the military junta has political and financial support from the Saudis and the Emiratis, it have little reason to back down. The protest leaders continue to be <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/AssociationSd/status/1136576283238813696\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">committed to nonviolence</a> despite another round of attacks by the Sudanese forces on Sunday to thwart a general strike. Sudan's opposition is <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/09/world/africa/sudan-protest-crackdown.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">embattled, hurting but defiant</a>.</p><p class=\"css-jwz2nf etfikam0\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/iyad_elbaghdadi\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">Iyad el-Baghdadi</a>, founder and president of the <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://kawaakibi.org\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">Kawaakibi Foundation</a>, a nonprofit working on the future of liberty in Arab and Muslim societies, is co-host of the podcast \"<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://arabtyrantmanual.com/podcast/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">The Arab Tyrant Manual</a>.\" </p><p class=\"css-jwz2nf etfikam0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">The Times is committed to publishing </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/01/31/opinion/letters/letters-to-editor-new-york-times-women.html\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">a diversity of letters</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> to the editor. We'd like to hear what you think about this or any of our articles. Here are some </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://help.nytimes.com/hc/en-us/articles/115014925288-How-to-submit-a-letter-to-the-editor\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">tips</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">. And here's our email: </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"mailto:letters@nytimes.com\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">letters@nytimes.com</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.</em></p><p class=\"css-jwz2nf etfikam0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Follow The New York Times Opinion section on </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.facebook.com/nytopinion\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Facebook</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">, </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"http://twitter.com/NYTOpinion\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Twitter (@NYTopinion)</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> and </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.instagram.com/nytopinion/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Instagram</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.</em></p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div></section>", "link": "/2019/06/11/opinion/sudan-protests.html", "title": "<title data-rh=\"true\">Opinion | The Princes Who Want to Destroy Any Hope for Arab Democracy - The New York Times</title>"}, "arizona-2020-vote": {"body": "<section class=\"meteredContent css-1i2y565\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" name=\"articleBody\"><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">CHANDLER, Ariz. \u2014 President Trump's <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/10/us/politics/immigration-mexico-deal-trump.html?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Homepage\" title=\"\">rancorous dispute with Mexico over tariffs</a> and the border energized some Republicans and rattled others around the country last week.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But in few places were the stakes as high as in Arizona, once as reliably Republican as it can get, now increasingly up for grabs. And the reactions played out like a preview of the divisions the party could face in presidential politics and a key Senate race in 2020.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Arizona's Chamber of Commerce, a proxy for the Republican establishment, predicted devastation if tariffs were placed on Mexican imports such as fruits and vegetables. Grass-roots Trump supporters \u2014 who are often at odds with the business community \u2014 stuck with the president.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"Who cares about the price of an avocado when we're having to pay for the illegals coming seeking asylum,\" said Dodie Bell, a small-business owner in the Phoenix suburb of Chandler, voicing support for the president the day Mr. Trump announced a deal with Mexico and withdrew the tariff threat.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><img alt=\"\" class=\"css-1m50asq\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" itemprop=\"url\" sizes=\"((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" srcset=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona2/merlin_156201357_2bfd10a7-b4a9-4ce2-931e-e0376f85388b-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w\"/></div><figcaption class=\"css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0\" itemprop=\"caption description\"><span class=\"css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0\">\"Who cares about the price of an avocado when we're having to pay for the illegals coming seeking asylum,\" said Dodie Bell, right, a small-business owner, with her husband, Steve Bell, in the Phoenix suburb of Chandler.</span><span class=\"css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90\" itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Credit</span><span>Ash Ponders for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But Tim Maiefski, a wealth manager out to dinner downtown, where restaurants sprayed cooling mists over outdoor tables, said that without immigrant labor, the state's economy would grind to a halt. Mr. Maiefski, who has always voted straight Republican, wants his party to nominate someone else in 2020. \"I hope to God they get somebody in there\" other than Mr. Trump, he said.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Those crosscurrents are on ample display in Chandler, a desert boomtown of 250,000 that reflects the demographic changes buffeting Arizona politics.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The city, once farmland where ostriches were raised, was the first in the country to <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.chandleraz.gov/news-center/chandler-first-nation-include-autonomous-vehicles-and-ride-sharing-zoning-code\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">rewrite its zoning code</a> to make way for self-driving cars. High-tech employers like Intel lure transplants from California, India and West Africa who have brought with them more liberal views.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Once staunchly Republican, many Chandler precincts were colored purple on a map of last year's midterms. Swing voters helped elect Democrats to the United States Senate, the secretary of state's office and, for the first time in the city's memory, the State Legislature.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Now, both parties believe that Arizona, which Mr. Trump <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/elections/2016/results/arizona?module=inline\" title=\"\">won by about 90,000 votes</a>, or 3.5 percentage points, is in play in the 2020 presidential race. Who wins will come down, in no small part, to places like Chandler, with its well-educated independent voters.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">[</em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/politics?smid=rd%3Faction%3Dclick&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Sign up for our politics newsletter</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> and join our conversation about the 2020 presidential race.]</em></p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Mr. Maiefski and his girlfriend, Tricia Mitchell, had passed a roofing crew in their neighborhood laboring outdoors in 103-degree heat. \"They work harder than anybody,\" Ms. Mitchell, an analyst for a tech firm, said of immigrants.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Ms. Mitchell did not vote in 2016. But she plans to show up next year \u2014 to cast a ballot against the president. She has a Hispanic daughter from an earlier relationship, and ever since Mr. Trump's election, she said, her daughter has faced racial hostility.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">To Democrats, the importance of making inroads in the Sun Belt followed the thunderclap of 2016, when Mr. Trump's appeal to white voters without a college degree won him Rust Belt states that had seemed permanently out of Republicans' reach.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If the president is able to hold on to even one of the so-called blue-wall states he won in 2016 \u2014 Michigan, Wisconsin and Pennsylvania \u2014 Democrats must find somewhere else to win.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">To many analysts, Arizona could be that somewhere else, thanks to the influx of college-educated transplants and the mobilization of Latino voters, especially young Latinos, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://kjzz.org/content/923981/2018-midterms-saw-massive-turnout-women-latino-and-young-voters\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">whose turnout in 2018</a> was higher than the 2016 presidential race \u2014 highly unusual for a midterm.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"This is not your dad's Arizona anymore,\" said Chuck Coughlin, a Republican consultant in the state. He said Midwest retirees, the traditional conservative base, were aging and being replaced by younger, more diverse and independent voters.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Recently, Mr. Coughlin sent a copy of a New York Times opinion column headlined \"<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/03/opinion/republicans-generation-gap.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">The Coming G.O.P. Apocalypse</a>\" to the Trump campaign's Arizona state director, Drew Sexton.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"I'd love to hear your thoughts on this column,\" he wrote. It noted that only 12 percent of millennials identify as mostly or consistently conservative.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"Got lots of thoughts,\" Mr. Sexton, himself a millennial, replied, according to Mr. Coughlin. \"Most of them bum me out.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Other Republican strategists said Arizona, which has not voted for a Democratic presidential candidate since 1996, is not about to flip next year.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"Every two years my friends on the left side of the spectrum declare that this is the election that will prove Arizona has become a purple swing state,\" said Stan Barnes, a former Republican state lawmaker who is now a lobbyist. \"Every year it ends up falling short.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">He said that even though Mr. Trump's approval was 45 percent in Arizona, with 51 percent disapproval, the Democratic nominee was likely to be outside the state's mainstream.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Most recently, former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr., the Democrat whom Arizona Republican strategists most fear, abandoned decades of support for a ban on federal money to pay for abortions.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"The Democratic Party seems determined to miss this moment and lurch to the left,\" Mr. Barnes said. \"They're going to be so far outside the spectrum of Arizona politics that Donald Trump becomes more attractive, even if you don't like his style and some of his policies.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Last year's Senate race, won by Kyrsten Sinema, a Democrat, is a lens through which both parties interpret the political lay of the land. Republicans argue that Ms. Sinema won by regularly voting for Trump priorities as a congresswoman, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.azcentral.com/story/news/politics/arizona/2018/04/01/kyrsten-sinema-tom-ohalleran-side-donald-trump-more-often-than-some-republicans/471206002/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">siding with the president</a> more than some Republicans \u2014 a centrism no Democratic presidential nominee would espouse.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/us/politics/11arizona4/merlin_146338272_cb940140-f53e-44b0-91c1-4ca480fc82ab-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><div class=\"css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0\">Last year's Senate race, won by Kyrsten Sinema, a Democrat, is a lens through which both parties interpret Arizona politics.</span><span class=\"css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90\" class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">To Democrats, Ms. Sinema successfully branded herself as an \"Arizonan,\" neither Democrat nor Republican, and her opponent, Martha McSally, damaged herself with independents by hugging Mr. Trump too tightly.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Ms. McSally, a former Air Force combat pilot, was later appointed to fill the Senate seat vacated when Senator John McCain died last year. She will probably be on the ballot again in 2020, likely facing Mark Kelly, a former astronaut, in one of the decisive races for Senate control.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">A factor that some Republicans fear could further alienate independents in both the presidential and Senate races is the resurgence of the far-right fringe in Arizona, which Mr. McCain had fought to marginalize.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">In January, the hard right elected as state Republican chairwoman Kelli Ward, whom national Republicans have attacked as a conspiracy theorist. Donations to the state party <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.facebook.com/KelliWardAZ/photos/a.172389932849085/2211405948947463/?type=3&amp;theater\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">have fallen sharply</a> under Ms. Ward. Recently, she <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.azcentral.com/story/news/politics/arizona/2019/05/20/how-extremists-reasserting-themselves-into-mainstream-gop-politics-arizona-republican-party/3568202002/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">shared a stage with far-right figures</a> at a suburban rally supported by three \"patriot\" organizations <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.splcenter.org/hatewatch/2019/04/12/four-things-know-about-next-weeks-patriotism-over-socialism-rally-arizona\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">identified as hate groups</a> by the Southern Poverty Law Center.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">In a telephone town hall last week, Ms. McSally faced a wave of criticism from grass-roots Republicans for breaking with Mr. Trump on his threat of Mexican tariffs, which the senator warned would harm the state economy.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"We're being invaded,\" parried a caller from Tucson, according to a transcript provided by a person who recorded the event. \"I don't see why the Republicans don't stick together on this.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">A second caller demanded, \"On the record today, are you going to support Donald Trump if he goes ahead with tariffs?\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Arizonans have more knowledge and experience of the porous border than most Americans do, and voters are sharply divided over Mr. Trump's harsh language about migrants, his demand for a wall and his handling of a surge in Central American families seeking asylum.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">John Giles, the Republican mayor of Mesa, said Mr. Trump would win the state again, but he cautioned, \"The president would be well served to soften his rhetoric when it comes to immigration if he wants to be successful in Arizona.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Voters in Chandler, as elsewhere, often fell into two camps: those who complain that taxpayers are providing services to undeserving, undocumented immigrants, and those who said migrants' economic contributions were substantial, and they deserved empathy.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"There's too many coming over, the state's taking care of millions, something's got to happen to stop it,\" said Eileen Cram, a retired school administrator, who moved to Chandler from Oregon.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">She plans to vote for Mr. Trump a second time. \"Somebody has to be in there that's strong, and he's strong \u2014 not wishy-washy,\" she said.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Another educator, Altreana Anderson, 43, a former principal of a primary school with a majority Hispanic population, said the real crisis was the failure to meet the health care needs of undocumented children and to offer legal work status to their parents, which traps families in poverty.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/sports/11arizona3/merlin_156201333_4550bb2d-85a6-41da-a171-102cfc6219dd-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><div class=\"css-zjzyr8\"><div data-testid=\"lazyimage-container\" style=\"height:257.77777777777777px\"></div></div></div><figcaption class=\"css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0\" itemprop=\"caption description\"><span class=\"css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0\">Altreana Anderson plans to vote against Mr. Trump for a second time in 2020, saying, \"He's been wrong since Day 1\" about immigration.</span><span class=\"css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90\" itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Credit</span><span>Ash Ponders for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"I do understand peoples' frustrations with them not paying taxes,\" Ms. Anderson said. \"If you gave them citizenship, they would pay into the system just as you and I do.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">She plans to vote against Mr. Trump for a second time in 2020. \"He's been wrong since Day 1,\" about immigration, accusing Mexico of sending criminals and rapists, she said.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"We are getting some very good people,\" she said. \"People I trust and love. We have no problem employing them. Walk into any neighborhood, and I can guarantee they have a landscaper who is undocumented.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Betty Iparraguirre, who voted for Mr. Trump, said she will not do so again.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"I thought because he wasn't a politician he would be different,\" said Ms. Iparraguirre, a real estate agent whose family is from Mexico.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">\"I can't understand half of what he's saying,\" she said. \"It's almost like watching a reality TV show. He's not even trying to fix something.\"</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div></section>", "link": "/2019/06/11/us/politics/arizona-2020-vote.html", "title": "<title data-rh=\"true\">Tariff Fight Reveals Republican Divisions in Up-for-Grabs Arizona - The New York Times</title>"}, "review-capitalist-robert-menasse": {"body": "<section class=\"meteredContent css-1i2y565\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" name=\"articleBody\"><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">It would be a mistake to call Robert Menasse's melancholy and satirical \"The Capital\" a great food novel. It has a lot more on its mind than breakfasts, lunches and dinners.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But it opens, after a prologue, with a question \u2014 \"Who invented mustard?\" \u2014 and takes off from there. By the next page, the author is describing \"staring at mustard on a plate while a sausage burns in the frying pan as a clear and typical symptom of depression.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Menasse's roomy novel is set in contemporary Brussels, and its characters can often be found consuming throwback comfort foods like <em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">stoemp</em>, a sausage and potato dish, and impenetrable stews such as <em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">waterzooi</em> and <em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">bigos</em>, and sighing with happiness. A lot of cigarettes are lit.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">It would be a mistake, too, to call \"The Capital\" a great novel of the European Union, though some have tried. (The book has received deservingly rapturous reviews abroad.) Menasse's novel is in part about bureaucratic infighting at the E.U., a subject he manages to make not just interesting but drolly comic.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Make no mistake: The author admires this institution and the notion of pushing past the nationalism that has tilted the world toward racism and war. But he can't help but satirize the vanities of his career-minded characters, with their little wheelie suitcases.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If you tasked an excellent writer with turning a tall stack of recent issues of The Economist into a novel, you might get \"The Capital.\" Somehow I mean this as high praise.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Born in 1954, Menasse is Austrian and well-known in Europe. He's published many books of fiction and nonfiction, and has received dozens of accolades. Few of his books have been translated into English, however. This is a situation, I predict, that will soon change.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Perhaps what we have in \"The Capital\" is a great murder mystery. The book opens with an assassination in a hotel, a murky, John le Carr\\xe9-world sort of event.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">It's an unusual murder story, though, because the suspense lies not in discovering the identity of the assassin (we follow him as he goes on the lam) but the identity of the dead man. You come to suspect that the murder is a kind of MacGuffin, that maybe it doesn't matter at all.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">I enjoyed \"The Capital\" so much that I could keep going like this. It's possible this is a great Holocaust-minded novel for a new millennium. Some of its characters are survivors of Auschwitz; others visit it. Still others think long and hard about the meanings to which the camp can be put as a symbol of the need for overcoming national sentiment.</p><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-11ih1w2 e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-zgakxe erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><img alt=\"\" class=\"css-1m50asq\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" itemprop=\"url\" sizes=\"50vw\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" srcset=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/books/review/10BOOKMENASSE2/10BOOKMENASSE2-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w\"/></div><figcaption class=\"css-1e7005o e1xdpqjp0\" itemprop=\"caption description\"><span class=\"css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0\">Robert Menasse</span><span class=\"css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90\" itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Credit</span><span>Rafaela Proell</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">One survivor meets yet another young person with a shaved head and tattoos and loses his cool. \"Did they know what they were doing, what they were expressing, what associations they were evoking?\" He thinks: \"You're insulting the world's memory!\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">In the end I think \"The Capital\" may be a major book about coincidences, of linked and overlapping meanings. G.K. Chesterton thought of coincidences as \"spiritual puns.\" For Menasse they have similar meaning. In this Balzac-like novel, he introduces us to a dozen or so characters \u2014 bureaucrats, police inspectors, academics, lobbyists \u2014 and spins them around one another. Their lives intersect and chime in resonant ways.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">One of these characters is Fenia Xenopoulou, a Greek woman turning 40 who has been given a job with no clout and almost no budget, as head of communications for the Directorate-General for Culture. She needs to get out. She kisses up, sleeping with a powerful colleague, and kicks down, ordering one of her subordinates, a sympathetic man named Martin Susman, to come up with an idea to help her consolidate her power.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">We meet an economist, Grace Atkinson, who hopes to escape the blame when Martin's idea goes awry. A police inspector, \\xc9mile Brunfaut, pushes back against a political cover-up of the hotel murder. An aging Austrian academic named Alois Erhart tells difficult truths about European nationalism, truths no one wants to hear.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Alois reminds us that everything is getting worse. The nature of truth has been degraded. Tabloids stir up base instincts and abet the rise of heinous old ghosts. \"He had been prepared for everything,\" we read, in a line made for the age of Donald Trump, \"but not everything in caricature.\"</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The heavy tourism in Brussels weighs on everyone, too. About \\xc9mile, the police inspector, Menasse writes: \"Back in 1914, his grandfather had said, Brussels was the richest and most beautiful city in the world \u2014 then they came three times, twice in their boots with rifles, the third time in their trainers with cameras.\"</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">This is a deeply humane novel, a novel for adults. It carries the wisdom and weight and weariness of late middle age. Menasse writes not about the way things should be but about the way things are, rare enough these days.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The translation, by Jamie Bulloch, is adroit. Yet \"The Capital\" made me want to learn to read in German, where it is surely even better.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">This is a baggy book, with room for everything Menasse wants to put into it. It has its share of longueurs. But there is also pointed writing not just about politics but about subways and orgasms and woolly underwear and air conditioning and retirement homes. You will learn a surprising amount about European pork production.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">W.H. Auden lamented, near the end of his life, that nothing he wrote saved a single Jew from the ovens. It's hard to reckon with the political impact a book like \"The Capital\" might have. I suspect it will be nil.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But this novel evidences a sharp awareness of the forces remaking European life, with Brexit as only one example. Ties are coming apart. To borrow a line from Ali Smith's recent novel, \"Spring,\" \"How're you doing, apart from the end of liberal capitalist democracy?\"</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div></section>", "link": "/2019/06/10/books/review-capitalist-robert-menasse.html", "title": "<title data-rh=\"true\">\u2018The Capital\u2019 Is a Sharp Political Satire About Europe at This Perilous Moment - The New York Times</title>"}, "police-dna-warrant": {"body": "<section class=\"meteredContent css-1i2y565\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" name=\"articleBody\"><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Someone broke into a church in Centerville, Utah, last November and attacked the organist who was practicing there. In March, after a conventional investigation came up empty, a police detective turned to forensic consultants at Parabon NanoLabs. Using the publicly accessible website GEDmatch, the consultants found a likely distant genetic relative of the suspect, whose blood sample had been found near the church's broken window.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"\" style=\"max-width:1200px;margin:0 auto\"><div class=\"large css-17dprlf\" data-id=\"100000006451534\" data-slug=\"privacy-mid-nav-module\" style=\"max-width:1050px\"><iframe class=\"css-sqdgy8\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/admin/100000006451534.embedded.html?\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></div></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Someone related to the person on GEDmatch did indeed live in Centerville: a 17-year-old high school student. Alerted by the police, a school resource officer watched the student during lunch at the school cafeteria and<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.deseretnews.com/article/900070489/plastic-milk-container-genealogy-helped-utah-police-crack-church-assault-case.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"> collected the milk carton and juice box</a> he'd thrown in the garbage. The DNA on the trash was a match for the crime scene evidence. This appears to be the<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.buzzfeednews.com/article/peteraldhous/genetic-genealogy-parabon-gedmatch-assault\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\"> first time</a> that this technique was used for an assault investigation.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The technique is known as genetic genealogy. It isn't simply a matter of finding an identical genetic match between someone in a database and evidence from a crime scene. Instead, a DNA profile may offer an initial clue \u2014 that a distant cousin is related to a suspect, for instance \u2014 and then an examination of birth records, family trees and newspaper clips can identify a small number of people for further investigation.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The identification of Joseph DeAngelo in the Golden State Killer case also relied on genetic genealogy. He was charged with<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/25/us/golden-state-killer-dna.html?module=inline\" title=\"\"> 26 counts of murder and kidnapping</a> after a genealogist helped investigators in California identify a third cousin of Mr. DeAngelo's through GEDmatch and other genealogical records.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">While there may be broad public support for a technique that solved serial murders, just because technology allows for a new type of investigation doesn't mean the government should be allowed to use it in all cases.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Genetic genealogy requires lots of DNA samples and an easy way to compare them. Americans have created millions of genetic profiles already. A 2018 study published in Science predicted that<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/11/science/science-genetic-genealogy-study.html?module=inline\" title=\"\"> 90 percent of Americans of European descent</a> will be identifiable from their DNA within a year or two, even if they have not used a consumer DNA service. As for easy access, GEDmatch's website provides exactly this opportunity. Consumers can take profiles generated from other commercial genetic testing services, upload them free and compare them to other profiles. So can the police.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">[If you use technology, someone is using your information. We'll tell you how \u2014 and what you can do about it. </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/privacy-project?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Sign up for our limited-run newsletter</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">.]</em></p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">We should be glad whenever a cold case involving a serious crimes like <!-- -->rape or murder<!-- --> can be solved. But the use of genetic genealogy in the Centerville assault case raises with new urgency fundamental questions about this technique.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">First, there is now no downward limit on what crimes the police might investigate through genetic genealogy. If the police felt free to use it in an assault case, why not shoplifting, trespassing or littering? </p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Second, there's the issue of meaningful consent. You may decide that the police should use your DNA profile without qualification and may even post your information online with that purpose in mind. But your DNA is also shared in part with your relatives. When you consent to genetic sleuthing, you are also exposing your siblings, parents, cousins, relatives you've never met and even future generations of your family. Legitimate consent to the government's use of an entire family tree should involve more than just a single person clicking \"yes\" to a website's terms and conditions.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Third, there's the question of why the limits on Americans' genetic privacy are being fashioned by private entities. The Centerville police used GEDmatch because the site owners allowed an exception to their own rules, which had permitted law enforcement access only for murder and sexual assault investigations. After user complaints, <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.gedmatch.com/tos.htm\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"\">GEDmatch expanded the list of crimes</a> that the police may investigate on its site to include assault. It also changed default options for users so that the police may not gain access to their profiles unless users affirmatively opt-in. But if your relative elects to do so, there's no way for you to opt out of that particular decision. And what's to stop GEDmatch from changing its policies again?</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Finally, the police usually confirm leads by collecting discarded DNA samples from a suspect. How comfortable should we be that a school resource officer hung around a high school cafeteria waiting to collect a teenager's \"abandoned\" <!-- -->DNA<!-- -->?</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">All of these issues point to one problem: Police use of genetic genealogy is virtually unregulated. Law enforcement agencies and cooperating genetic genealogy websites are operating in a world of few limits. There are not only few rules about which crimes to investigate, but also unclear remedies in the case of mistakes, the discovery of embarrassing or intrusive information, or misuse of the information. </p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If these concerns sounds similar to other technology and privacy problems we're facing, they should. Our genetic and digital identities raise similar questions of autonomy, civil liberties, and intrusion by public and private entities.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Without legal limits, genetic genealogy will become a more popular tool for the police. Rather than wait for the courts to deal with difficult and novel issues about genetic surveillance and privacy, state legislatures and attorneys general should step in and articulate guidelines on how far their law enforcement agencies should go. Congress and the Federal Trade Commission should take further steps to protect the privacy and security of consumer genetic data. </p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">If the police are to be given unlimited access to the genetic information of your entire family tree, they should have it at the end of a public debate, not by default.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Like other media companies, The Times collects data on its visitors when they read stories like this one. For more detail please see </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://help.nytimes.com/hc/en-us/articles/115014892108-Privacy-policy?module=inline\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">our privacy policy</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> and </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/10/opinion/sulzberger-new-york-times-privacy.html?rref=collection%2Fspotlightcollection%2Fprivacy-project-does-privacy-matter&amp;action=click&amp;contentCollection=opinion&amp;region=stream&amp;module=inline&amp;version=latest&amp;contentPlacement=8&amp;pgtype=collection\" title=\"\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">our publisher's description</em></a><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\"> of The Times's practices and continued steps to increase transparency and protections.</em></p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><em class=\"css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0\">Follow </em><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://twitter.com/privacyproject\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" Get a Warrant - The New York Times</title>"}, "master-recordings-universal-fire": {"body": "<section class=\"meteredContent css-1i2y565\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" name=\"articleBody\"><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Eleven years ago this month, a fire ripped through a part of Universal Studios Hollywood.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\"><a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2008/06/01/us/01cnd-fire.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">At the time</a>, the company said that the blaze had destroyed the theme park's \"King Kong\" attraction and a video vault that contained only copies of old works.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">But, according to an <a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/11/magazine/universal-fire-master-recordings.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">article published on Tuesday by The New York Times Magazine</a>, the fire also tore through an archive housing treasured audio recordings, amounting to what the piece described as \"the biggest disaster in the history of the music business.\"</p><h2 class=\"css-ani50b eoo0vm40\" id=\"link-3fee16dc\">What happened?</h2><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The fire started in the early hours of June 1, 2008.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Overnight, maintenance workers had used blowtorches to repair the roof of a building on the set of New England Street, a group of colonial-style buildings used in scenes for movies and television shows. The workers followed protocol and waited for the shingles they worked on to cool, but the fire broke out soon after they left, just before 5 a.m.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The flames eventually reached Building 6197, known as the video vault, which housed videotapes, film reels and, crucially, a library of master sound recordings owned by Universal Music Group. </p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn\"><div class=\"css-53u6y8\"><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Hundreds of firefighters responded to the fire. After trying to douse it with foam retardant and water from a nearby lake, emergency personnel decided to dismantle the warehouse containing the vault to more easily put out the fire.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">[<a class=\"css-1g7m0tk\" href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/11/magazine/universal-fire-master-recordings.html?module=inline\" title=\"\">Read the full investigation here: \"The Day The Music Burned.</a>\"]</p><h2 class=\"css-ani50b eoo0vm40\" id=\"link-3f20b16e\">What was lost?</h2><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">Almost all of the master recordings stored in the vault were destroyed in the fire, including those produced by some of the most famous musicians since the 1940s. </p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">In a confidential report in 2009, Universal Music Group estimated the loss at about 500,000 song titles.</p><p class=\"css-exrw3m evys1bk0\">The lost works most likely included masters in the Decca Records collection by Billie Holiday, Louis Armstrong, Duke Ellington, Al Jolson, Bing Crosby, Ella Fitzgerald and Judy Garland. The fire probably also claimed some of Chuck Berry's greatest recordings, produced for Chess Records, as well as the masters of some of Aretha Franklin's first appearances on record.</p></div><aside class=\"css-o6xoe7\"></aside></div><div class=\"css-79elbk\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper\"><div class=\"css-z3e15g\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-wrapper-hidden\"></div><div class=\"css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15\" data-testid=\"photoviewer-children\"><figure aria-label=\"media\" class=\"css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0\" itemid=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/magazine/11xp-music2/a4859d27382c48f2b3c1c8c80b91ff68-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp\" itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" role=\"group\"><div class=\"css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0\"><span class=\"css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0\">Image</span><img alt=\"\" class=\"css-1m50asq\" sizes=\"((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw\" src=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/magazine/11xp-music2/a4859d27382c48f2b3c1c8c80b91ff68-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale\" srcset=\"https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/06/11/magazine/11xp-music2/a4859d27382c48f2b3c1c8c80b91ff68-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp
